Multiply and accumulate With K=128: - 128 multiplies and adds per (t, s) pair - 2016 (t, s) pairs total - 2016 * 128 * 2 = 516K FLOPs per chunk This is tiny. In Triton, I can vectorize over K. For each t: ``` w_t = beta[t] * a[t] # (K,) vector for s in range(t): d_ts = D[t, s] # scalar w_s = load w[s] # (K,) vector w_t -= beta[t] * d_ts * w_s # wait, beta[t] is already in w_t initialization? No. ``` Hmm wait. Let me re-derive. From the forward substitution: ``` (I + L) @ w = beta * exp(g) * k = beta * a ``` where L[t,s] = beta[t] * D[t,s]. ``` w[t] + sum_{s < t} beta[t] * D[t,s] * w[s] = beta[t] * a[t] w[t] = beta[t] * (a[t] - sum_{s < t} D[t,s] * w[s]) ``` So the beta[t] factors out: ``` correction = 0 for s in range(t): correction += D[t, s] * w[s] w[t] = beta[t] * (a[t] - correction) ``` In this form, w[t] depends on w[s] but NOT multiplied by beta[t] in the sum. The beta[t] is applied once at the end. Let me double-check: L[t,s] = beta[t] * D[t,s]. So: w[t] + sum_{s<t} beta[t] * D[t,s] * w[s] = beta[t] * a[t] w[t] = beta[t] * a[t] - beta[t] * sum_{s<t} D[t,s] * w[s] w[t] = beta[t] * (a[t] - sum_{s<t} D[t,s] * w[s]) Yes, this is correct! So in the code: ``` correction = sum_{s<t} D[t,s] * w[s] # w[s] already includes beta[s] w[t] = beta[t] * (a[t] - correction) ``` Great, so the implementation is: ``` for t in range(BT): # Compute correction = sum_{s < t} D[t,s] * w[s] correction = zeros(K) for s in range(t): d_ts = D[t, s] correction += d_ts * w[s] w[t] = beta[t] * (a[t] - correction) ``` For u, it's the same but with v instead of a: ``` for t in range(BT): correction = zeros(V) for s in range(t): d_ts = D[t, s] correction += d_ts * u[s] u[t] = beta[t] * (v[t] - correction) ``` OK, let me now write the Triton kernel. I'll be very careful. For the inter-chunk recurrence, I'll also write a Triton kernel. Actually, let me think about a simpler approach for the inter-chunk kernel. Since NT is only 16-64, the inter-chunk recurrence loop has at most 64 iterations. Actually, let me just try a different approach: can I vectorize the intra-chunk recurrence? Looking at the recurrence again: ``` for i in range(1, BT): A[i, :i] += matmul(A[i:i+1, :], A[:, :i]) ``` This is: for each row i, update the first i columns using information from all columns and all previous rows. This is inherently sequential but can be expressed as one operation per row. 63 iterations of batched matmul, each on (B, H, NT, 1, BT) @ (B, H, NT, BT, i). Actually, there's a known technique: the recurrence A += A @ A can be computed in O(log BT) steps rather than O(BT) steps. Specifically: A_1 = A A_2 = A_1 + A_1 @ A_1 A_4 = A_2 + A_2 @ A_2 ... Each step doubles the number of terms in the series. After ceil(log2(BT)) ≈ 6 steps, we have A + A^2 + ... + A^{2^k}. Since A is strictly lower triangular, A^{64} = 0 for a 64×64 matrix. So 6 steps (2^6 = 64) is enough! Let me verify: Step 0: A_0 = A (has terms up to A^1) Step 1: A_1 = A_0 + A_0 @ A_0 = A + A^2 (has terms up to A^2) Step 2: A_2 = A_1 + A_1 @ A_1 = (A + A^2) + (A + A^2) @ (A + A^2) = (A + A^2) + (A^2 + 2A^3 + A^4) = A + 2A^2 + 2A^3 + A^4 But wait, that's not right. We want (I - A)^{-1} - I = A + A^2 + A^3 + ... Actually, let me reconsider. The recurrence A_new = A + A @ A (not A += A @ A) doesn't compute the right thing. Let me define S_k = A + A^2 + ... + A^{2^k}. Then: S_0 = A S_{k+1} = S_k + S_k @ S_k Wait, S_k @ S_k = (A + A^2 + ... + A^{2^k}) @ (A + A^2 + ... + A^{2^k}) = A^2 + A^3 + ... + A^{2^{k+1}} + cross terms The cross terms are things like A^2 @ A^3 = A^5, which give additional contributions beyond what we want. So this doesn't give exact (I-A)^{-1} - I. However, for our purposes (numerical correctness within 0.05 tolerance), an approximation might work. But the reference computes it exactly, so I need to match it. Hmm, actually, there's a linear recurrence approach. For a strictly lower triangular matrix A: Let X_k = I + A + A^2 + ... + A^{k-1}. Then: X_1 = I X_2 = I + A X_4 = X_2 * (I + A^2) = (I + A)(I + A^2) = I + A + A^2 + A^3 Wait, that's not right either because A and A^2 have the triangular property but (I+A)(I+A^2) = I + A + A^2 + A*A^2. And A*A^2 = A^3 (since matrix multiplication is associative). So X_{2k} = X_k * (I + A^k). This is the prefix-sum doubling approach. We can compute: X_1 = I + A (has terms up to A^1) A_1 = A For step in range(log2(BT)): A_{next} = A_cur @ A_cur (square the matrix) X_{next} = X_cur @ (I + A_{next}) Wait, let me think again. We want X = (I - A)^{-1} = I + A + A^2 + A^3 + ... Note that (I - A)(I + A + A^2 + ... + A^{n-1}) = I - A^n. For a nilpotent A (A^{BT} = 0), we have (I - A)(I + A + ... + A^{BT-1}) = I. So X = I + A + A^2 + ... + A^{BT-1}. We can compute this efficiently: X = I P = A while any(P != 0): X = X + P P = P @ A But this still takes O(BT) steps. Alternatively, with doubling: Let B_k = A^{2^k} Let S_k = I + A + A^2 + ... + A^{2^k - 1} Then: B_0 = A S_0 = I + A (wait, S_0 should be I + A for k=0: terms up to A^{1} = A^{2^0}) Hmm, for k=0: terms up to A^{1-1} = A^0 = I? No. Let me define: S_k = sum_{i=0}^{2^k - 1} A^i. Then S_0 = I (just A^0). S_{k+1} = sum_{i=0}^{2^{k+1} - 1} A^i = sum_{i=0}^{2^k - 1} A^i + sum_{i=2^k}^{2^{k+1} - 1} A^i = S_k + A^{2^k} * sum_{j=0}^{2^k - 1} A^j = S_k + A^{2^k} * S_k And A^{2^{k+1}} = A^{2^k} * A^{2^k}. So the doubling recurrence is: A_0 = A S_0 = I For k = 0 to ceil(log2(BT))-1: S_{k+1} = S_k + A_k @ S_k A_{k+1} = A_k @ A_k After K = ceil(log2(BT)) steps: S_K = I + A + A^2 + ... + A^{2^K - 1} Since A^{BT} = 0, we want S_K where 2^K >= BT, so K = 6 for BT=64. Then (I - A)^{-1} - I = S_K - I (since S_K includes I). So the computation is: S = I P = A for k in range(6): # ceil(log2(64)) = 6 S = S + P @ S # S_k + A^{2^k} * S_k P = P @ P # A^{2^{k+1}} Result = S - I Wait, this doesn't match the reference recurrence. Let me verify with a simple 2×2 matrix: A = [[0, 0], [a, 0]] (strictly lower triangular, BT=2) A^2 = [[0, 0], [0, 0]] (zero) Reference method: - A_init = [[0, 0], [-a, 0]] (negated and masked) - i=1: A[1, :1] += A[1, :] @ A[:, :1] = A[1, 0] += A[1, :1] * A[:1, :1] = -a += (-a * 0) = -a - A = [[0, 0], [-a, 0]] - A = (A + I) = [[1, 0], [-a, 1]] - Then A_final = A * beta (column scaling) (I - A_init_w_sign)^{-1} where A_init_w_sign = [[0, 0], [-a, 0]]: I - A_init_w_sign = I + [[0, 0], [a, 0]] = [[1, 0], [a, 1]] Inverse = [[1, 0], [-a, 1]] So (I - A_init_w_sign)^{-1} - I = [[0, 0], [-a, 0]] = A_new. ✓ Now my doubling method with A_work = -A_init (the negated version): A_work = [[0, 0], [a, 0]] (since -(-beta[t]*D[t,i]) = beta[t]*D[t,i]... hmm wait) Let me use the original A from the reference (before negation): A_orig[t,i] = beta[t] * D[t,i] for t > i. After negation: A_ref[t,i] = -beta[t] * D[t,i] for t > i. This is A_ref = -L where L[t,i] = beta[t]*D[t,i]. The recurrence on A_ref computes (I - A_ref)^{-1} - I = (I + L)^{-1} - I. My doubling method: with A_work = L = -A_ref: P_0 = L S_0 = I After k steps: S_k = I + L + L^2 + ... + L^{2^k-1} With 6 steps: S_6 = I + L + L^2 + ... + L^{63} (since 2^6 = 64) S_6 - I = L + L^2 + ... + L^{63} = (I - L)^{-1} - I - L^{64} - ... But L^{64} = 0 for 64×64 strictly lower triangular, so S_6 - I = (I - L)^{-1} - I. But we want (I + L)^{-1} - I, not (I - L)^{-1} - I. Are these the same? No! (I + L)^{-1} ≠ (I - L)^{-1} unless L^2 = 0. Hmm. So the doubling method with L doesn't compute the right thing. Let me reconsider. The recurrence in the reference computes: B = (I - A_ref)^{-1} - I where A_ref = -L. But (I - A_ref) = (I + L). So B = (I + L)^{-1} - I. The doubling method I described computes: S = I + M + M^2 + ... = (I - M)^{-1} for M = whatever matrix I use. If I set M = -L, then (I - M)^{-1} = (I + L)^{-1}. ✓ So P_0 = -L = A_ref. S_0 = I. After 6 doubling steps: S_6 = I + A_ref + A_ref^2 + ... + A_ref^{63} = (I - A_ref)^{-1} = (I + L)^{-1}. S_6 - I = (I + L)^{-1} - I. ✓ This matches! The doubling on the NEGATED matrix (A_ref, which is -beta[t]*D[t,i]) gives the correct result. Let me verify with the 2×2 example: A_work = A_ref = [[0, 0], [-a, 0]] (this is -L where L[t,i] = beta[t]*D[t,i]) P_0 = [[0, 0], [-a, 0]] S_0 = I = [[1, 0], [0, 1]] Step 1: S_1 = S_0 + P_0 @ S_0 = I + A_ref = [[1, 0], [-a, 1]] P_1 = P_0 @ P_0 = [[0, 0], [-a, 0]] @ [[0, 0], [-a, 0]] = [[0, 0], [0, 0]] Step 2: S_2 = S_1 + P_1 @ S_1 = [[1, 0], [-a, 1]] + 0 = [[1, 0], [-a, 1]] P_2 = 0 Result: S_2 - I = [[0, 0], [-a, 0]] = A_ref. ✓ Great! The doubling method works. And it takes only 6 iterations (ceil(log2(64))) instead of 63. Each iteration is a matmul: (B, H, NT, BT, BT) @ (B, H, NT, BT, BT). OK, let me now rewrite the solution with this optimization. Plan: 1. Compute a = k * exp(g_cumsum), b = k * exp(-g_cumsum) 2. D = a @ b^T → (B, H, NT, BT, BT) — one batched GEMM 3. A_ref = -beta_row * D, zero upper tri + diag → (B, H, NT, BT, BT) 4. Compute (I + L)^{-1} - I using doubling (log2(64) = 6 steps) - P = A_ref, S = I - For k in range(6): S = S + P @ S P = P @ P - result = S - I 5. A_final = (result + I) * beta_col = S * beta_col 6. w = A_final @ (exp(g) * k) = S @ (beta_col * exp(g) * k) Wait, A_final = S * beta_col. So w = (S * beta_col) @ x where x = exp(g) * k. But S * beta_col applies column scaling, so (S * beta_col) @ x = S @ (beta_col ⊙ x). w = S @ (beta_col.unsqueeze(-1) * exp(g) * k) 7. u = S @ (beta_col.unsqueeze(-1) * v) Step 6: S is (B, H, NT, BT, BT). RHS = beta.unsqueeze(-1) * exp(g) * k is (B, H, NT, BT, K). w = S @ RHS: (B, H, NT, BT, BT) @ (B, H, NT, BT, K) = (B, H, NT, BT, K). ✓ This is much more efficient! Only 6 + 2 = 8 batched matmuls for the intra-chunk, instead of 63 + 2. Let me implement this. I'll use PyTorch for now and measure performance. Actually wait, step 4's doubling has an issue. After a few iterations, A_ref^k should become zero for k >= BT (strictly lower triangular nilpotent). But the doubling computes P = P @ P, which means P at step k is A_ref^{2^k}. After step 6, P = A_ref^{64} = 0. But the intermediate S matrices might not be strictly lower triangular. Actually, S = I + A_ref + A_ref^2 + ... = (I - A_ref)^{-1}. This matrix is unit lower triangular (1s on diagonal, nonzero below diagonal). So S @ S is still unit lower triangular. Both S and P remain well-behaved. Let me code this up. I'll keep the PyTorch approach but with the log-recurrence optimization. Actually, wait. I realize I don't even need the doubling for the matrix series. Let me think about whether there's an even simpler approach. For a strictly lower triangular matrix L, (I + L)^{-1} exists and is also lower triangular. Since BT=64 is small, I could directly compute the inverse using forward substitution (solving I + L for each column of I). But that's the same as the forward substitution for w and u, just applied to a basis. Actually, the doubling approach with 6 steps is the most efficient. Let me implement it. But wait, there's another issue. The doubling involves matrix multiplications: (B, H, NT, BT, BT) @ (B, H, NT, BT, BT). For BT=64, this is 64×64 matmuls, which are small. With B*H*NT=512 batches, the total is 512 * 64^3 = 134M FLOPs per matmul. With 6 doubling steps, that's 6*2 = 12 matmuls (S update + P update per step), so ~1.6G FLOPs. This is still small. But actually, PyTorch's bmm/batched matmul might not be the most efficient for 512 batches of 64×64 matmuls. The overhead might be high. Hmm, let me just try it and see. First, let me implement the doubling approach, then benchmark. OR, even better, let me write a Triton kernel for the intra-chunk computation that processes one chunk at a time, using tl.dot for all matmuls. Each chunk does: - D = a @ b^T (one tl.dot, 64×128 × 128×64) - 6 steps of doubling (6 tl.dot calls, each 64×64 × 64×64) - w = S @ RHS_w (one tl.dot, 64×64 × 64×128) - u = S @ RHS_u (one tl.dot, 64×64 × 64×128) This is 1+6+2 = 9 matmuls per chunk. With B*H*NT=512 chunks, that's 4608 small matmuls. This is a lot of kernel launches in a loop. If I use PyTorch batched operations instead: - D = a @ b^T: one batched GEMM, 512 batches - 6 doubling steps: 12 batched GEMMs, 512 batches each - w and u: 2 batched GEMMs, 512 batches each Total: 15 batched GEMM kernel launches 15 kernel launches is much better than 4608! OK, let me just use PyTorch batched operations with the log-recurrence optimization. Let me rewrite _intra_chunk_pytorch.

Still very slow — ~0.0005× peak. The doubling reduced intra-chunk from 63 to 6 iterations, but we still have the inter-chunk recurrence loop (NT=16-64 iterations) that launches many PyTorch operations. The bottleneck is now likely: 1. The inter-chunk recurrence loop: NT iterations, each launching ~5 batched matmul kernels. For NT=64, that's ~320 kernel launches. 2. The intra-chunk: 6 doubling steps, each with 2 batched matmuls = 12 matmuls. Plus D computation, w, u matmuls = +3. Total ~15 matmuls. With overhead ~1ms total. The total kernel launch overhead for ~330 kernel launches is significant. The actual compute is tiny (few GFLOPS), so it's all overhead. To really speed this up, I need to: 1. Fuse the inter-chunk recurrence into a single Triton kernel (NT loop inside the kernel) 2. Possibly fuse intra-chunk as well Let me write proper Triton kernels. For the inter-chunk recurrence, I'll write a kernel that processes all chunks for one (B, H) pair, using tl.dot for matmuls. Actually, wait. The inter-chunk recurrence loop over NT is parallel across (B, H). There are up to 16 (B, H) pairs, each processing 64 chunks. If I write a Triton kernel with grid=(B*H,), each program does 64 chunk iterations, each with several tl.dot calls. The key matmuls inside the inter-chunk loop: 1. w_i @ S: (BT, K) @ (K, V) = (BT, V) 2. qg @ S: (BT, K) @ (K, V) = (BT, V) 3. qg @ kg_inv^T: (BT, K) @ (K, BT) = (BT, BT) [Aqk] 4. Let me think about how to structure this kernel efficiently. The key challenge is managing registers and shared memory for the S state and intermediate results. Approach: For each chunk iteration inside the kernel: 1. Load q, k, g, w, u for this chunk from global memory 2. Compute qg = q * exp(g), kg_inv = k * exp(-g) 3. Compute v_eff using tl.dot for w @ S 4. Compute o_i using tl.dot for qg @ S and Aqk @ v_eff 5. Update S using tl.dot for k_scaled^T @ v_eff For S (K, V) = (128, 128): keep in registers (64KB). This is a lot but should work with 128+ threads. For loading chunk data: load in tiles. q, k are bf16 (2 bytes), g is fp32 (4 bytes). Actually, let me think about what tl.dot looks like for each matmul. I'll use the approach where I compute each matmul as a loop over the contraction dimension. For w @ S: w is (BT, K), S is (K, V). I need (BT, V) result. - Input A: w, shape (BT, K) — load as (BT_TILE, K_TILE) blocks - Input B: S^T, shape (V, K) — load as (V_TILE, K_TILE) blocks - tl.dot(A, B) = (BT_TILE, V_TILE) - Loop over BT and V output tiles, with K as contraction dim For the state update: k_scaled^T @ v_eff: k_scaled is (K, BT), v_eff is (BT, V). I'll be practical about it. Actually, instead of writing a big fused inter-chunk kernel, let me first try to optimize the PyTorch inter-chunk recurrence. The Python loop over NT iterations launches many kernels. Can I reduce the number of kernel launches? Looking at the inter-chunk recurrence: ``` for i in range(NT): qg = q_i * exp(g_i) kg_inv = k_i * exp(-g_i) Aqk = matmul(qg, kg_inv^T) Aqk.masked_fill_(upper, 0) v_eff = u_i - matmul(w_i, S) o_i = matmul(qg, S) + matmul(Aqk, v_eff) S = S * exp(g_i_last) + matmul(k_scaled^T, v_eff) ``` Each iteration has 4 matmul calls. For NT=64, that's 256 matmul calls per (B,H), times B*H=16, gives 4096 matmul launches. Plus elementwise ops. Way too many! The key issue: matmul with small batch dimension (B*H=16) doesn't utilize the GPU well. What if I batch over chunks too? The matmuls per iteration: - matmul(qg, kg_inv^T): (B, H, BT, K) @ (B, H, K, BT) = (B, H, BT, BT) Can't batch over NT since Aqk depends on intra-chunk data only. - matmul(w_i, S): (B, H, BT, K) @ (B, H, K, V) = (B, H, BT, V) This COULD be batched over NT if S were the same for all chunks. The only way to speed it up is to fuse everything into a single kernel that does the NT loop internally. Let me write a Triton kernel for the inter-chunk recurrence. uv run python -c "
import torch
import math

# Quick numerical test: doubling recurrence for (I - A)^{-1} where A is lower tri
BT = 8
A = torch.randn(BT, BT).tril(-1) * 0.5  # random strict lower triangular
A_ref = -A
I = torch.eye(BT)

# Reference: sequential
A_seq = A_ref.clone()
for i in range(1, BT):
    A_seq[i, :i] += (A_seq[i:i+1, :] @ A_seq[:, :i]).squeeze(0)
result_seq = (A_seq + I) @ torch.randn(BT, 5)  # apply to random RHS

# Doubling: compute (I - A_ref)^{-1} = I + A_ref + A_ref^2 + ...
P = A_ref.clone()
S = I.clone()
n_steps = math.ceil(math.log2(BT))
for _ in range(n_steps):
    S = S + P @ S
    P = P @ P
result_double = S @ torch.randn(BT, 5)  # S = (I - A_ref)^{-1}

print('Seq result:', result_seq[:3, 0])
print('Double result:', result_double[:3, 0])
print('Max diff:', (result_seq - result_double).abs().max().item())
# Should be ~0
" 2>&1

Let me trace through more carefully. Actually, the doubling formula I derived was: P_0 = A, S_0 = I S_{k+1} = S_k + P_k @ S_k P_{k+1} = P_k @ P_k After K steps, S = I + A + A^2 + ... + A^{2^K - 1} But the reference recurrence is: For i from 1 to BT-1: A[i, :i] += (A[i, :] @ A[:, :i])[None, :i] This is: A_new[i, j] = A[i, j] + sum_m A[i, m] * A[m, j] for j < i. This is: A_new = A + A^2? Let me check... Actually, the recurrence uses the UPDATED A values. It's NOT A + A^2, it's more complex. For a strictly lower triangular A: - When we update A[1, 0]: there are no intermediate m since A[1, m] = 0 for m >= 1 and A[m, 0] = 0 for m <= 0. So A[1, 0] stays the same. - When we update A[2, 0]: A[2, 0] += A[2, 1] * A[1, 0]. Here A[2, 1] is the ORIGINAL value (not updated yet since we're processing row 2 and column 1 hasn't been updated). Actually wait: A[2, 1] is from the original A, and A[1, 0] is also from the original A (since row 1 was already processed but A[1,0] wasn't changed). So A[2, 0] += A_orig[2,1] * A_orig[1,0]. - When we update A[3, 0]: A[3, 0] += A[3, 1] * A[1, 0] + A[3, 2] * A[2, 0] Here A[3, 1] is the original, A[1, 0] is the original, A[3, 2] is the original, but A[2, 0] has been UPDATED! A[2, 0] = A_orig[2, 0] + A_orig[2, 1] * A_orig[1, 0] So: A[3, 0] += A_orig[3,1]*A_orig[1,0] + A_orig[3,2]*(A_orig[2,0] + A_orig[2,1]*A_orig[1,0]) = A_orig[3,1]*A_orig[1,0] + A_orig[3,2]*A_orig[2,0] + A_orig[3,2]*A_orig[2,1]*A_orig[1,0] = A_orig^2[3,0] + A_orig^3[3,0] So the recurrence computes A + A^2 + A^3 + ... = (I-A)^{-1} - I, but it uses a specific order that ensures each term is computed exactly once. The doubling method should give the same result. Let me debug my test... Actually, in my test, I used: ```python A = torch.randn(BT, BT).tril(-1) * 0.5 A_ref = -A I = torch.eye(BT) # Sequential A_seq = A_ref.clone() for i in range(1, BT): A_seq[i, :i] += (A_seq[i:i+1, :] @ A_seq[:, :i]).squeeze(0) result_seq = (A_seq + I) @ torch.randn(BT, 5) # Doubling P = A_ref.clone() S = I.clone() for _ in range(math.ceil(math.log2(BT))): S = S + P @ S P = P @ P result_double = S @ torch.randn(BT, 5) ``` Wait, the sequential reference applies to A, and then computes `(A_seq + I) @ RHS`. Let me think about why... OH WAIT. I see the issue! In the sequential reference: ```python A_seq[i, :i] += (A_seq[i:i+1, :] @ A_seq[:, :i]).squeeze(0) ``` This uses A_seq[i, :] which includes columns >= i too (but those are all zero since A is lower tri). And A_seq[:, :i] only uses columns 0..i-1. But `A_seq[i:i+1, :] @ A_seq[:, :i]` = sum_m A_seq[i, m] * A_seq[m, :i] for all m. Since A_seq[i, m] = 0 for m >= i (lower triangular), this sum only includes m < i. And A_seq[m, :i] includes columns j < i (matching). This should be the same as the matrix product. Let me add more debugging... Actually, let me re-examine. The reference code for the sequential recurrence is: ```python A[..., i, :i] += (A[..., i, :, None] * A[..., :, :i]).sum(-2) ``` Wait, this uses elementwise multiply (*) and sum, not matmul! Let me look at this more carefully. `A[..., i, :, None]` has shape (..., BT, 1) — row i, all columns, with extra dim. `A[..., :, :i]` has shape (..., BT, i) — all rows, first i columns. When multiplied elementwise: (..., BT, 1) * (..., BT, i) → (..., BT, i) [broadcasting dim 1 over dim BT]. Then sum(-2): sum over the BT dimension, giving (..., i). So this computes: for each j < i, sum_m A[i, m] * A[m, j]. This IS the matrix product! In my test, I used `(A_seq[i:i+1, :] @ A_seq[:, :i]).squeeze(0)` which should give the same result. Let me check with a concrete example... Hmm wait, actually in my SOLUTION CODE, I used: ```python inc = torch.matmul(A[..., i:i+1, :], A[..., :, :i]).squeeze(-2) ``` This is a matmul, not elementwise multiply + sum. And I just verified above that the elementwise multiply + sum IS equivalent to matmul. But wait, the reference uses: ```python (A[..., i, :, None] * A[..., :, :i]).sum(-2) ``` This gives shape (..., i) after the sum. And `torch.matmul(A[..., i:i+1, :], A[..., :, :i])` gives shape (..., 1, i), which after squeeze(-2) gives (..., i). These should be identical. Let me trace through a concrete 3x3 example manually to find the discrepancy... A_init (lower tri, no diag): [0 0 0] [a10 0 0] [a20 a21 0] Reference recurrence: i=1: A[1, :1] += sum_m A[1, m] * A[m, :1] = A[1, 0] += sum_m A[1, m] * A[m, 0] = a10 += A[1, 0] * A[0, 0] + A[1, 1] * A[1, 0] + A[1, 2] * A[2, 0] = a10 += 0 + 0 + 0 = a10 (unchanged) i=2: A[2, :2] += sum_m A[2, m] * A[m, :2] = A[2, 0] += A[2,0]*A[0,0] + A[2,1]*A[1,0] + A[2,2]*A[2,0] = 0 + a21*a10 + 0 = a21*a10 = A[2, 1] += A[2,0]*A[0,1] + A[2,1]*A[1,1] + A[2,2]*A[2,1] = 0 + 0 + 0 = 0 (unchanged) So A after recurrence: [0 0 0] [a10 0 0] [a20+a21*a10 a21 0] Now doubling method: P_0 = A = [[0,0,0],[a10,0,0],[a20,a21,0]] S_0 = I = [[1,0,0],[0,1,0],[0,0,1]] Step 1: S_1 = S_0 + P_0 @ S_0 = I + A = [[1,0,0],[a10,1,0],[a20,a21,1]] P_1 = P_0 @ P_0 = A @ A A @ A = [[0,0,0],[a10,0,0],[a20,a21,0]] @ [[0,0,0],[a10,0,0],[a20,a21,0]] = [[0,0,0], [0,0,0], [a21*a10, 0, 0]] Step 2: S_2 = S_1 + P_1 @ S_1 = S_1 + A^2 @ (I + A) = [[1,0,0],[a10,1,0],[a20,a21,1]] + [[0,0,0],[0,0,0],[a21*a10,0,0]] @ [[1,0,0],[a10,1,0],[a20,a21,1]] = [[1,0,0],[a10,1,0],[a20,a21,1]] + [[0,0,0],[0,0,0],[a21*a10,0,0]] = [[1,0,0],[a10,1,0],[a20+a21*a10,a21,1]] S_2 = I + A + A^2 + A^3 (since A^3 = 0 for 3x3) (A_seq + I) from reference = [[1,0,0],[a10,1,0],[a20+a21*a10,a21,1]] These match! ✓ So the doubling should work. Let me debug my test code more carefully... Actually, in my test, I used: ```python A = torch.randn(BT, BT).tril(-1) * 0.5 A_ref = -A ``` So A_ref is the NEGATIVE of a strictly lower triangular matrix. This is what goes into the recurrence. Then the reference computes (I - A_ref)^{-1} - I + I = (I - A_ref)^{-1} = (I + A)^{-1}. And the doubling computes S = (I - A_ref)^{-1} = (I + A)^{-1}. But wait, (I - A_ref) = (I + A) since A_ref = -A. And (I + A) is not the same as (I - A)! So the doubling formula for (I - A)^{-1} uses P_0 = A, but in my case A_ref = -A, so P_0 should be A_ref not A. In my test: P = A_ref.clone() = -A. And S = I. Step 1: S = I + P@I = I + A_ref = I - A. Step 2: S = (I - A) + A_ref^2 @ (I - A) = (I - A) + A^2 @ (I - A) [since A_ref^2 = (-A)^2 = A^2] = I - A + A^2 - A^3 Hmm, that doesn't look right. Wait, A_ref^2 = (-A)^2 = A^2. And A_ref^2 @ (I + A_ref) = A^2 @ (I - A). So S_2 = (I - A) + A^2 @ (I - A) = (I + A^2) @ (I - A) = I - A + A^2 - A^3. But we want (I - A_ref)^{-1} = (I + A)^{-1} = I - A + A^2 - A^3 + A^4 - ... (alternating signs!). And (I - A)^{-1} = I + A + A^2 + A^3 + ... (all positive). So (I - A_ref)^{-1} = (I + A)^{-1} ≠ (I - A)^{-1}. They have different series expansions! The doubling formula gives (I - P_0)^{-1} where P_0 is the starting matrix. For P_0 = A_ref = -A, we get (I + A)^{-1}, which IS what we want! For P_0 = A, we get (I - A)^{-1}, which is NOT what we want. So P_0 should be A_ref = -A, not A. Let me check my test again... In my test: `P = A_ref.clone()` where `A_ref = -A`. So P_0 = A_ref = -A. S = I, P = A_ref. Step 1: S = I + P@I = I + A_ref = I - A. P = P@P = A_ref^2 = A^2. Step 2: S = (I + A_ref) + A^2 @ (I + A_ref) = (I - A) + A^2 - A^3. P = A^4. After step 2 (2 steps total for BT=8, log2(8)=3): Wait, I said `n_steps = math.ceil(math.log2(BT))` which for BT=8 is 3. S_3 = S_2 + P_2 @ S_2 = (I - A + A^2 - A^3) + A^4 @ (I - A + A^2 - A^3) = I - A + A^2 - A^3 + A^4 - A^5 + A^6 - A^7 For a strictly lower triangular 8x8 matrix, A^8 = 0, so S_3 = I - A + A^2 - A^3 + A^4 - A^5 + A^6 - A^7. This IS the correct Neumann series for (I + A)^{-1}: (I + A)^{-1} = I - A + A^2 - A^3 + A^4 - ... ✓ And the reference computes (I - A_ref)^{-1} = (I + A)^{-1}. ✓ So my doubling test should be correct! Let me re-examine my test code... ```python A = torch.randn(BT, BT).tril(-1) * 0.5 A_ref = -A I = torch.eye(BT) # Sequential A_seq = A_ref.clone() for i in range(1, BT): A_seq[i, :i] += (A_seq[i:i+1, :] @ A_seq[:, :i]).squeeze(0) result_seq = (A_seq + I) @ torch.randn(BT, 5) # Doubling P = A_ref.clone() S = I.clone() for _ in range(math.ceil(math.log2(BT))): S = S + P @ S P = P @ P result_double = S @ torch.randn(BT, 5) ``` Hmm wait, the sequential code computes: ``` A_seq = A_ref (which is -A) for i in range(1, BT): A_seq[i, :i] += (A_seq[i:i+1, :] @ A_seq[:, :i]).squeeze(0) result_seq = (A_seq + I) @ RHS ``` This computes `A_seq = (I - A_ref)^{-1} - I`, then `result_seq = (A_seq + I) @ RHS = (I - A_ref)^{-1} @ RHS`. The doubling computes `S = (I - P_0)^{-1}` where `P_0 = A_ref`, so `S = (I - A_ref)^{-1}`. And `result_double = S @ RHS`. These should be the same! Let me check the random RHS: ```python result_seq = (A_seq + I) @ torch.randn(BT, 5) result_double = S @ torch.randn(BT, 5) ``` I'm using different random RHS vectors! That explains the difference! The RHS is randomly generated and different each time. Let me fix by using the same RHS. Oh, I see the bug in my test. Each `torch.randn(BT, 5)` call generates a different random tensor. Let me fix: ```python RHS = torch.randn(BT, 5) result_seq = (A_seq + I) @ RHS result_double = S @ RHS ``` But in my test, the third line after the sequential uses the first `torch.randn` and the doubling line uses a second `torch.randn`. That's the discrepancy! OK so the doubling approach IS mathematically correct. The test was just using different RHS vectors. import torch
from reference import _naive_chunk_kda, get_inputs
from solution import _kda_forward

torch.manual_seed(42)
inputs = get_inputs()
q, k, v, g, beta = [t.cuda() for t in inputs]
scale = 128 ** -0.5

with torch.no_grad():
    ref_o = _naive_chunk_kda(q.clone(), k.clone(), v.clone(), g.clone(), beta.clone(), scale, chunk_size=64)
    sol_o = _kda_forward(q.clone(), k.clone(), v.clone(), g.clone(), beta.clone(), scale, chunk_size=64)

diff = (ref_o.float() - sol_o.float()).abs()

print(f'mean diff: {diff.mean():.6f}')
